What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Soundness Conditions and Refutation of Alternatives
Exemplification of sound matter and form and refutation of alternative definitions of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for sound reasoning and how the commentator refutes alternative interpretations based on movement and attention.

Core Idea
Sound reasoning requires both sound matter and sound form, not mere movement.

After defining nazar as a tartib with matter and form, the commentator gives concrete examples and addresses potential misunderstandings. He emphasizes that soundness depends on both components being sound, and he refutes two alternative views: one that sees reasoning as movement and another as mere attention.

Guided Path
1
Examine the examples
Read the examples for conceptions and assents to see what constitutes sound matter and form.
These are concrete illustrations.
2
Analyze the refutation of the movement view
The author argues that if nazar were movement, its soundness wouldn't depend on matter and form. Consider why this is problematic.
3
Analyze the refutation of the attention view
Similarly, the attention view ignores the role of premises. Understand the flaw.
4
Understand the clarification about 'bi-manzila'
The commentator explains why 'bi-manzila' (in the position of) is used.
This addresses a potential confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله لتأدي إلى مجهول ولا شك أن هذا الترتيب يتعلق بشيئين أحدهما تلك العلوم التي يقع فيها الترتيب وهي بمنزلة المادة له والثاني تلك الهيئة المترتبة عليه وهي بمنزلة الصورة له، فإذا اتصف كل واحدة منهما بما هو صحتها في نفسها اتصف الترتيب قطعاً بصحته في نفسه، أعني تأديته إلى المطلوب، وإلا فلا وهذا معنى قوله ( ولكل ترتيب مادة وصورة أي لا بد له من أمرين يجريان منه مجرى المادة والصورة من ) المركب منهما ( فتكون ) جواب لما الفاء مع وهو قليل في الاستعمال (صحته ) أي صحة النظر بمعنى تأديته إلى المطلوب ) بصحة المادة ) أي بسبب صحتها، أما في التصورات فمثل أن يكون المذكور في موضع الجنس مثلاً جنساً لا عرضاً عاماً، وفي موضع الفصل فصلا لا خاصة وفي موضع الخاصة خاصة شاملة بينة، وأما في التصديقات فمثل أن تكون القضايا المذكورة في الدليل مناسبة للمطلوب وصادقة إما قوله : ( ولا شك إلخ ) أي هذا الترتيب الذي هو فعل الناظر يتعلق بشيئين أحدهما بمنزلة المادة في كون الترتيب به بالقوة والثاني بمنزلة الصورة في حصوله به بالفعل، فإذا اتصف كل واحد بما هو صحته في نفسه اتصف الترتيب بالصحة التي هي صفته بخلاف ما إذا كان عبارة عن الحركتين، لأن الحركة حاصلة بالفعل من مبدأ المسافة أعني المطلوب المشعور به بوجه إلى منتهاها أعني الوجه المجهول وليست بالقوة عند حصول العلوم، وبالفعل عند حصول الهيئة فلا تكون صحة النظر، حينئذ بصحة المادة والصورة بل بترتب ما لأجله الحركة أعني حصول العلوم المناسبة والهيئة المنتجة وبخلاف ما إذا كان عبارة عن التوجه المذكور فإن العلوم السابقة لا مدخل لها في التأدية حينئذ، فلا تكون صحته لصحة المادة والصورة أيضا، وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما في شرح المقاصد من أنه يستفاد من عبارة المواقف ابتناء انقسام النظر إلى الصحيح والفاسد، باعتبار المادة والصورة على تفسيره بالترتيب وليس كذلك ولبعض الناظرين لبيان الابتناء المذكور توجيه بعيد عن العبارة لا يقبله الطبع السليم. قوله : ( وهي بمنزلة المادة إلخ ) زاد لفظ بمنزلة لعدم كونهما ركنين للترتيب، ولأن المادة والصورة مختصة بالأجسام، والوجه الأخير ذكره الشارح في حاشيته الكبرى والصغرى، التخصيص مستنداً بأن العلة المادية والصورية شاملة للجواهر والأعراض، منشؤه والاعتراض بمنع عدم الفرق بين المادة والصورة والعلة المادية والصورية فلا تكن من الخابطين. قوله : (بسبب صحتها يعني أن الباء للسببية لا للملابسة، حتى يكون المعنى صحته باعتبار صحتها فيكون وصفه بها باعتبار حال متعلقه أي صحيح مادتها وصورتها على ما في شرح المقاصد . وحاشيته الصغرى توجيهاً لذلك القول أن الفكر عرض لا مادة له ولا صورة، ففيه بحث لأن المفهوم من إطلاقاتهم في مباحث العلة والمعلول عموم العلة الصورية والمادية بحسب الاصطلاح للجواهر والأعراض كما سيأتي إن شاء الله . المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : أنه يؤدي إلى المطلوب وفاسد يقابله ٢١٣ قوله : (إما قطعاً إلخ) مفعول مطلق أي صدق قطع أو ظن أو تسليم أو حال، أي مقطوعة أو مظنونة أو مسلمة وهذا تقسيم باعتبار الصناعات الثلاثة المعتبرة في تحصيل المطالب النظرية، أعني البرهان والخطابة والجدل وأسقط المغالطة والشعر لعدم إفادتهما المجهول . قوله : (مجتمعتين) إشارة إلى أن كلمة معاً حال وليس ظرفاً بمعنى في وقت واحد .
The fifth observatory - the second objective: that it (nazar) leads to the desired conclusion and its opposite is unsound. For it leads to the unknown. There is no doubt that this ordering (tartib) relates to two things: one is those sciences in which the ordering occurs, and they are like the matter (madda) for it; the second is that arrangement (hay'a) which is ordered upon it, and it is like the form (sura) for it. When each of them is characterized by what is its soundness in itself, the ordering is necessarily characterized by its own soundness, i.e., its leading to the desired conclusion; otherwise not. This is the meaning of his statement: (And every ordering has matter and form, i.e., it must have two things that proceed from it in the manner of matter and form from the composite of both. So (it becomes) the answer to 'lam' with 'fa'—and its usage is rare— (its soundness) i.e., the soundness of nazar in the sense of its leading to the desired (conclusion) (by the soundness of the matter) i.e., due to its soundness. As for conceptions (tasawwurat), for example, that what is mentioned in the place of the genus is a genus, not a common accident; and in the place of the differentia, it is a differentia, not a proprium; and in the place of the proprium, it is a comprehensive and evident proprium. As for assents (tasdiqat), for example, that the propositions mentioned in the proof (dalil) are appropriate to the desired conclusion and true, either... His statement: (And there is no doubt, etc.) i.e., this ordering, which is the act of the inquirer (nazir), relates to two things: one is in the position of matter in that the ordering is potential through it; the second is in the position of form in that it is actualized through it. When each is characterized by what is its soundness in itself, the ordering is characterized by the soundness that is its attribute, unlike if it were expressed by two movements, because the movement is actually obtained from the starting point of the distance, i.e., the desired known in some way, to its endpoint, i.e., the unknown face, and it is not potential when the sciences are obtained, but actual when the arrangement is obtained. Thus, the soundness of nazar then is not due to the soundness of matter and form, but rather due to the ordering for the sake of which the movement occurs, i.e., the obtaining of appropriate sciences and the productive arrangement. And unlike if it were expressed by the aforementioned attention (tawajjuh), for the prior sciences have no role in the leading then; hence its soundness is not due to the soundness of matter and form either. By what we have mentioned, what is in Sharh al-Maqasid is refuted: that it is understood from the expression of al-Mawaqif that the division of nazar into sound and unsound is based on matter and form according to its interpretation as ordering, and this is not so. Some inquirers, to clarify the aforementioned foundation, have a direction far from the expression that a sound disposition does not accept. His statement: (And they are in the position of matter, etc.) He added the word 'position' (bi-manzila) because they are not pillars (rukn) for the ordering, and because matter and form are specific to bodies. The latter point was mentioned by the commentator in his major and minor glosses, the specification being based on the fact that material and formal causes encompass substances and accidents. Its origin and the objection by denying the difference between matter and form and material and formal cause—so do not be among the confused. His statement: (due to its soundness) means that the 'ba' is for causality, not for accompaniment, so that the meaning is: its soundness by virtue of its soundness, so its description is by virtue of the state of its object, i.e., its matter and form are sound, as in Sharh al-Maqasid and his minor gloss, as a direction for that statement: that thought (fikr) is an accident with no matter or form. In it there is investigation because the understood from their usages in the topics of cause and effect is the generality of formal and material cause in terminology for substances and accidents, as will come, God willing. The fifth observatory - the second objective: that it (nazar) leads to the desired conclusion and its opposite is unsound. His statement: (either qat'an, etc.) is an absolute object, i.e., truth of certainty (qat'), or opinion (zann), or concession (taslim), or a state, i.e., being certain, opined, or conceded. This is a division according to the three arts (sina'at) considered in obtaining theoretical aims, namely demonstration (burhan), oratory (khataba), and dialectic (jadal). He omitted sophistry (mugalata) and poetry (shi'r) because they do not benefit the unknown. His statement: (both together (ma'an)) indicates that the word 'ma'an' is a state (hal) and not an adverb meaning 'at one time'.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-condition passage. The commentator explains the nature of correct reasoning (nazar) as an ordering (tartib) that leads from known premises to an unknown conclusion. He identifies two essential components of this ordering: the matter (madda), which consists of the sciences (premises) that serve as raw material, and the form (sura), which is the arrangement (hay'a) of those premises. The soundness (sihha) of the reasoning depends on the soundness of both matter and form. If either is corrupt (fasid), the reasoning is unsound. The commentator then discusses the types of premises: they must be true either by certainty (qat'), opinion (zann), or concession (taslim), corresponding to the three arts of demonstration, oratory, and dialectic. He also refutes an interpretation that nazar's soundness is based on movement or attention, emphasizing instead the interdependence of matter and form. Finally, he clarifies that the word 'ma'an' (together) in the phrase 'بسبب صحتها معاً' indicates a state, not a temporal adverb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sound reasoning is simply moving from known to unknown.
Correction: The passage shows that soundness requires sound matter and form, not just movement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of sound matter in assents?
A) Propositions that are appropriate and true
B) Propositions that are certain
C) Propositions that are generally accepted
D) Propositions that are from authority

Key Takeaway
Sound reasoning requires both correct premises (matter) and correct arrangement (form); alternative views that ignore this are refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are convinced by an argument about your faith, do you ever pause to examine whether its premises are true (sound matter) and its logical structure is valid (sound form)? Or do you rely on the feeling of moving from known to unknown? What is one belief you hold that you have never examined in this way? Will you examine it today?
?
The passage warns against being among the confused (khabitin). In which aspect of your Islamic practice are you most likely to accept imprecise reasoning—mixing up categories like genus and differentia? How will you protect yourself from this confusion starting this week?
?
If you had to defend a core Islamic doctrine to a skeptic, would your reasoning have sound matter (accurate premises from revelation and reason) and sound form (valid inference)? Or would it be mere 'movement' or 'attention'? What specific step will you take to strengthen your reasoning in that area?

What Is Kalam?
beginner

Types of Premises: Certainty, Opinion, and Concession
Division of nazar's premises by certainty, opinion, and concession, and mapping to three arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the premises of nazar are divided into certain, opinion, and concession, corresponding to the three arts of demonstration, oratory, and dialectic.

Core Idea
The premises of reasoning are certain, opinion-based, or conceded, matching three logical arts.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the premises used in nazar can be of three types: certain (qat'i), opinion (zanni), or concession (taslimi), each corresponding to a specific art of reasoning. He also notes that sophistry and poetry are excluded because they do not lead to the unknown, and he clarifies the grammatical function of the word 'ma'an'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of premises
Find the terms qat'an, zannan, tasliman in the passage.
They correspond to certainty, opinion, and concession.
2
Connect to the three arts
The passage links these to burhan, khataba, jadal. Understand the purpose of each.
3
Notice the omission of sophistry and poetry
They are excluded because they do not benefit the unknown.
Reflect on why.
4
Understand the grammatical point
The word 'ma'an' is a state, not an adverb.
This clarifies the phrase.

وصورتها على ما في شرح المقاصد . وحاشيته الصغرى توجيهاً لذلك القول أن الفكر عرض لا مادة له ولا صورة، ففيه بحث لأن المفهوم من إطلاقاتهم في مباحث العلة والمعلول عموم العلة الصورية والمادية بحسب الاصطلاح أو مسلمة وهذا تقسيم باعتبار الصناعات الثلاثة المعتبرة في تحصيل المطالب النظرية، أعني البرهان والخطابة والجدل وأسقط المغالطة والشعر لعدم إفادتهما المجهول . قوله : (مجتمعتين) إشارة إلى أن كلمة معاً حال وليس ظرفاً بمعنى في وقت واحد .
The fifth observatory - the second objective: that it (nazar) leads to the desired conclusion and its opposite is unsound. For it leads to the unknown. There is no doubt that this ordering (tartib) relates to two things: one is those sciences in which the ordering occurs, and they are like the matter (madda) for it; the second is that arrangement (hay'a) which is ordered upon it, and it is like the form (sura) for it. When each of them is characterized by what is its soundness in itself, the ordering is necessarily characterized by its own soundness, i.e., its leading to the desired conclusion; otherwise not. This is the meaning of his statement: (And every ordering has matter and form, i.e., it must have two things that proceed from it in the manner of matter and form from the composite of both. So (it becomes) the answer to 'lam' with 'fa'—and its usage is rare— (its soundness) i.e., the soundness of nazar in the sense of its leading to the desired (conclusion) (by the soundness of the matter) i.e., due to its soundness. As for conceptions (tasawwurat), for example, that what is mentioned in the place of the genus is a genus, not a common accident; and in the place of the differentia, it is a differentia, not a proprium; and in the place of the proprium, it is a comprehensive and evident proprium. As for assents (tasdiqat), for example, that the propositions mentioned in the proof (dalil) are appropriate to the desired conclusion and true, either... His statement: (And there is no doubt, etc.) i.e., this ordering, which is the act of the inquirer (nazir), relates to two things: one is in the position of matter in that the ordering is potential through it; the second is in the position of form in that it is actualized through it. When each is characterized by what is its soundness in itself, the ordering is characterized by the soundness that is its attribute, unlike if it were expressed by two movements, because the movement is actually obtained from the starting point of the distance, i.e., the desired known in some way, to its endpoint, i.e., the unknown face, and it is not potential when the sciences are obtained, but actual when the arrangement is obtained. Thus, the soundness of nazar then is not due to the soundness of matter and form, but rather due to the ordering for the sake of which the movement occurs, i.e., the obtaining of appropriate sciences and the productive arrangement. And unlike if it were expressed by the aforementioned attention (tawajjuh), for the prior sciences have no role in the leading then; hence its soundness is not due to the soundness of matter and form either. By what we have mentioned, what is in Sharh al-Maqasid is refuted: that it is understood from the expression of al-Mawaqif that the division of nazar into sound and unsound is based on matter and form according to its interpretation as ordering, and this is not so. Some inquirers, to clarify the aforementioned foundation, have a direction far from the expression that a sound disposition does not accept. His statement: (And they are in the position of matter, etc.) He added the word 'position' (bi-manzila) because they are not pillars (rukn) for the ordering, and because matter and form are specific to bodies. The latter point was mentioned by the commentator in his major and minor glosses, the specification being based on the fact that material and formal causes encompass substances and accidents. Its origin and the objection by denying the difference between matter and form and material and formal cause—so do not be among the confused. His statement: (due to its soundness) means that the 'ba' is for causality, not for accompaniment, so that the meaning is: its soundness by virtue of its soundness, so its description is by virtue of the state of its object, i.e., its matter and form are sound, as in Sharh al-Maqasid and his minor gloss, as a direction for that statement: that thought (fikr) is an accident with no matter or form. In it there is investigation because the understood from their usages in the topics of cause and effect is the generality of formal and material cause in terminology for substances and accidents, as will come, God willing. The fifth observatory - the second objective: that it (nazar) leads to the desired conclusion and its opposite is unsound. His statement: (either qat'an, etc.) is an absolute object, i.e., truth of certainty (qat'), or opinion (zann), or concession (taslim), or a state, i.e., being certain, opined, or conceded. This is a division according to the three arts (sina'at) considered in obtaining theoretical aims, namely demonstration (burhan), oratory (khataba), and dialectic (jadal). He omitted sophistry (mugalata) and poetry (shi'r) because they do not benefit the unknown. His statement: (both together (ma'an)) indicates that the word 'ma'an' is a state (hal) and not an adverb meaning 'at one time'.

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-condition passage. The commentator explains the nature of correct reasoning (nazar) as an ordering (tartib) that leads from known premises to an unknown conclusion. He identifies two essential components of this ordering: the matter (madda), which consists of the sciences (premises) that serve as raw material, and the form (sura), which is the arrangement (hay'a) of those premises. The soundness (sihha) of the reasoning depends on the soundness of both matter and form. If either is corrupt (fasid), the reasoning is unsound. The commentator then discusses the types of premises: they must be true either by certainty (qat'), opinion (zann), or concession (taslim), corresponding to the three arts of demonstration, oratory, and dialectic. He also refutes an interpretation that nazar's soundness is based on movement or attention, emphasizing instead the interdependence of matter and form. Finally, he clarifies that the word 'ma'an' (together) in the phrase 'بسبب صحتها معاً' indicates a state, not a temporal adverb.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all reasoning must yield certainty.
Correction: The passage acknowledges that opinion and concession are valid in their respective arts.

Checkpoint
Which two arts are omitted from the classification of reasoning?
A) Sophistry and poetry
B) Demonstration and oratory
C) Dialectic and sophistry
D) Oratory and poetry

Key Takeaway
The premises of reasoning can be certain, opinion-based, or conceded, matching three arts: demonstration, oratory, and dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your discussions about Islam, do you often expect certainty from others in matters where only opinion or concession is appropriate? How can you adjust your expectations to be more just?
?
When you encounter a difficult theological issue, do you prefer the comfort of concession (taslim) over the effort of seeking demonstration (burhan)? What is one area you could commit to studying more deeply?
?
Have you ever used emotional rhetoric (like poetry) or deceptive arguments (like sophistry) to 'win' a religious debate? How can you ensure your pursuit of truth remains honest and grounded in sound reasoning?

Sound Inference and Its Conditions
Al-Iji defines sound inference and its opposite, listing conditions for soundness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sound inference and the two conditions (soundness of matter and form) that determine its validity.

Core Idea
Sound inference requires both true premises and correct logical structure.

Al-Iji begins this objective by establishing what makes an inference sound. An inference leads to the desired conclusion either definitively, by opinion, or by admission. Its soundness depends on two inseparable conditions: the truth of its matter (premises) and the correctness of its form (logical arrangement). Corruption in either or both invalidates the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining sound inference
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the definition and conditions.
Notice that soundness is tied to both matter and form.
2
Identify the two conditions
Find where the text mentions soundness of matter (premises true) and soundness of form (logical structure). Understand that both are necessary.
The corruption of either one alone is enough to corrupt the inference.
3
Reflect on the categories of conclusion
Note that the inference leads to the desired conclusion either definitively (qat'an), by opinion (zannan), or by admission (tasliman). This corresponds to demonstration, rhetoric, and dialectic.

والخطابة والجدل وأسقط المغالطة والشعر لعدم إفادتهما المجهول . قوله : (مجتمعتين) إشارة إلى أن كلمة معاً حال وليس ظرفاً بمعنى في وقت واحد . قوله : ( لأن النظر إلخ) يعني أن جلاء النظر وخفاءه إنما هو بالنظر إلى بيانه وكشفه للمنظور فيه وهو لا يجامعه أصلاً لكونه معداً له فلا يتصف بصفاته . قوله : ( وصادقة إما قطعاً أو ظناً أو تسليماً) أي صادقة في نفس الأمر أما حال كونه مقطوعة أو مظنونة أو مسلمة لا أن ثبت له أي لا دليل له يدل على ثبوته
The Fifth Observatory - The Second Objective: That it leads to the desired [conclusion] and the corrupt opposite of it, definitively, or by opinion, or by admission; and due to the soundness of the form resulting from observing the conditions considered in the arrangement of definitions and proofs together—i.e., due to these two soundnesses combined—and its corruption is due to the corruption of both together or the corruption of only one of them. And among them is one who divided it—i.e., inference—into the clear and the hidden. But this is far-fetched because inference is a thing by which explanation is sought and does not accompany it, so it cannot be described by what are attributes of explanation. Therefore, he verified it and said: 'And its verification is that the proof may be characterized by the two modalities—meaning clarity and hiddenness—in two respects: one according to the form, which is the shape that arises from the premises, for the syllogistic figures differ in clarity and hiddenness in entailing the desired conclusion, for the first figure does not need a middle term in that, while others need a middle term, fewer or more; and the second according to the matter, for the desired conclusion may depend on many premises—and more—that is, by the desired conclusion not being initially based on necessary premises, but ending at them through intermediaries of varying degrees in multiplicity—and few and fewer—that is, by being based on necessities, for example, through one intermediary or being based on them initially, despite their difference—i.e., the difference of the premises in clarity and hiddenness, even if they are necessary—due to the variation in abstracting the two extremes as its explanation has passed. And you are aware that the difference according to matter also applies to the definition, for its parts may be necessary varying in clarity, and may be theoretical ending at necessities through one intermediary or intermediaries, unlike the difference according to form. His saying: 'either definitively etc.' is an absolute object, i.e., truth of certainty, or opinion, or admission, or a state, i.e., being certain, or opinion-based, or admitted. And this is a division according to the three arts considered in acquiring theoretical aims, namely demonstration, rhetoric, and dialectic; he omitted sophistry and poetry because they do not yield the unknown. His saying: 'both together' is an indication that the word 'together' is a state and not an adverb meaning 'at one time'. His saying: 'because inference etc.' means that the clarity and hiddenness of inference is only with respect to its explaining and revealing the object of inference, and it does not accompany it at all because it is prepared for it, so it cannot be described by its attributes. His saying: 'and true either definitively or by opinion or by admission' means true in reality, while being certain, or opinion-based, or admitted, not that its truth is according to these matters; otherwise, soundness would not be determined. And it is also indicated by his saying in what preceded, otherwise opinion about correspondence would be necessary, so reflect. His saying: 'and does not accompany it' means neither by both being together in something nor by inference being described as explanation, as he stated in Abkār al-Afkār. His saying: 'unlike the difference according to form, for if you say: the difference according to it also occurs by presenting the more general or delaying it, I say: delaying the more general, although permissible in definition, but usage is definitively on presenting it, so there is no difference according to it in the definitions current among them, unlike the proof, so the difference became apparent... according to form, therefore he specifically mentioned the proof. And if it is intended by clarity and hiddenness of inference that which we have mentioned, then it does not apply to inference in reality but to the proof or definition, and metaphorical usage does not prevent it; rather, it is permissible to describe inference by what are attributes of the object of inference, and this metaphorical usage is what occurs in their speech such as 'this is a clear inference' and 'that is a hidden inference'. And if something else is intended by clarity and hiddenness of inference, i.e., other than what we have mentioned, then there is no proof for it, i.e., no evidence indicating its establishment.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage defines sound inference and its opposite, then refutes the division of inference into clear and hidden. The author begins by stating that inference leads to the desired conclusion either definitively, by opinion, or by admission, and its soundness depends on two things: soundness of matter (the premises being true) and soundness of form (the arrangement meeting logical conditions). Corruption occurs when either or both are corrupt. Then he mentions that some scholars divide inference into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi). He rejects this because inference is a tool to attain explanation and does not itself have the attribute of clarity or hiddenness—these belong to the proof (dalil) or the explanation. The correct analysis is that the proof (dalil) can have these two modalities from two perspectives: (1) form (sura), i.e., the logical figure—the first figure is clearest, others require additional middle terms; (2) matter (madda), i.e., the premises—some are more hidden because they depend on many intermediaries before reaching necessary premises. The commentary explains each part: 'either definitively etc.' is a division based on the three arts (demonstration, rhetoric, dialectic). 'Both together' emphasizes simultaneity as a condition, not time. It clarifies that inference cannot be described as clear or hidden because it is the means, not the end. The passage concludes that if clarity and hiddenness are taken as attributes of the proof, it is allowed to apply them metaphorically to inference, but if taken literally in another sense, there is no evidence.

Key Terms
نظر (inference)
صورة (form)
مادة (matter)
مقدمات (premises)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an inference is sound if only the premises are true, regardless of logical form.
Correction: The passage clearly states that soundness requires both soundness of matter and form together. Corruption of either one suffices to corrupt the inference.

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions for sound inference according to the passage?
A) Soundness of matter and soundness of form
B) Soundness of matter and soundness of conclusion
C) Soundness of form and soundness of terms
D) Soundness of premises and soundness of middle term

Key Takeaway
Sound inference requires both true premises and valid logical form; corruption of either invalidates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief with true premises but flawed logic? How does this lesson challenge you to check your reasoning in your daily life? Will you commit to examining both truth and form before accepting an argument?
?
Which type of conclusion do you usually seek in matters of faith—certainty, strong opinion, or mere acceptance? Are you willing to be honest with yourself about what level of conviction your arguments actually provide?
?
When you find yourself convinced of a religious argument, do you ever examine both the truth of its premises and the validity of its form? What might you have accepted wrongly because you only checked one? Who can help you hold your reasoning to this standard?

Why Inference Cannot Be Clear or Hidden
Al-Iji refutes dividing inference into clear/hidden and explains how proof can have these attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing inference into clear and hidden is rejected, and how clarity and hiddenness properly apply to proof from two perspectives.

Core Idea
Clarity and hiddenness belong to the proof, not the act of inference.

After defining sound inference, Al-Iji addresses a proposed division of inference into clear and hidden. He firmly rejects this because inference is a tool for seeking explanation, not something that can be clear or hidden itself. However, he verifies that the proof (dalil) may be described as clear or hidden from two aspects: its syllogistic form and the nature of its premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of dividing inference
Read the passage where the author says this division is far-fetched because inference seeks explanation and does not accompany it.
The key phrase: 'inference is a thing by which explanation is sought and does not accompany it'.
2
Understand the verification
Read the verification that the proof can be clear or hidden from two respects: form and matter.
The first figure is clearest in form; premises with fewer intermediaries are clearer in matter.
3
Note the metaphorical application
At the end, the author acknowledges that clarity/hiddenness can be attributed to inference metaphorically, based on the proof's attributes.

Why does the author consider dividing inference into clear and hidden far-fetched?

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you reflect on your own beliefs about Allah, do you tend to think that your reasoning process itself is 'clear' or 'hidden'? How does this lesson force you to instead examine the proofs you rely on, rather than your mental act of inferring?
?
Consider an area of your faith where you feel uncertain. Can you trace the premises of your reasoning back to necessary truths (like the Quran and Sunnah), or have you unknowingly built your understanding on many intermediaries that may be flawed? What concrete step can you take to verify the foundational proofs?
?
Al-Iji says clarity and hiddenness belong to the proof, not the inference. Does this make you more humble about your intellectual abilities? How willing are you to admit that your own thinking might be 'hidden' because the proof itself is complex, even if you feel certain? Commit to one way you will test the soundness of your proofs this week.

What is Sound Inference?
Al-Iji defines sound inference and clarifies its requirements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji defines sound inference and why he defines it by its conditions (matter and form) rather than by its effect of yielding knowledge.

Core Idea
Sound inference is defined by its causes, not its result.

In this passage, al-Iji establishes the definition of sound inference as accepted by the majority of Islamic theologians. The definition focuses on the conditions that make inference sound – its matter and form – rather than simply describing it as leading to knowledge. This subtle shift avoids circular reasoning and grounds the claim that sound inference yields knowledge as a self-evident truth. The passage also clarifies that sound inference must yield at least opinion, and that the statement 'it yields knowledge' is taken as universal in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: definition, then explanation, then two additional clarifications.
The brackets in the original mark the section heading.
2
Identify the definition of sound inference
Find the part that defines sound inference as 'the one that includes its conditions according to its matter and form yields knowledge of the object of inference.' Notice the key terms: matter (مادة) and form (صورة).
3
Understand why the definition uses conditions
The commentator explains that defining sound inference by its conditions (rather than by its effect) avoids circularity. If we said 'sound inference is that which leads to the desired conclusion,' then saying it yields knowledge would be a tautology. By specifying the conditions, the implication of knowledge becomes self-evident.
This is a key methodological point in Islamic theology.
4
Note the additional points about opinion and universality
The passage states that sound inference's yielding of opinion is agreed upon, and that the earlier statement about knowledge is a general statement that in the sciences is treated as universal.
These clarify the scope of the definition.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: How does this definition help establish the validity of theological reasoning? Why is it important to define inference by its conditions rather than its effects?
This connects to the broader goal of grounding theological knowledge in sound reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور ] المشتمل على شرائطه بحسب مادته وصورته ( يفيد العلم بالمنظور فيه (عند الجمهور ( وأما إفادته للظن فقد قيل : إنها متفق عليها عند الكل ( ولا بد ) قبل الشروع قوله : ( المشتمل على شرائطه إلخ) فسر صحة النظر بما هو سببها ليترتب الحكم عليه بإفادته للعلم من غير شبهة بل يكون بديهياً على ما نقله من نهاية العقول لا لأنه لا تفسيرها بما هو صفة؛ إذ لا خفاء في صحة قولنا النظر الذي يؤدي إلى حصول المطلوب يفيد العلم به في الجملة ولأنه لو كان كذلك لكان تقسيمه إلى القسمين باعتبار التأدية وعدمها عبثاً. قوله : (متفق عليها إلخ) لأنه لو لم يكن مفيداً للظن أيضاً لم يكن مؤدياً إلى حصول المطلوب أصلاً لا علماً ولا ظناً فلا يكون صحيحاً قوله : ( ولا بد إلخ) فإن المذكور سابقاً مهملة تحتمل الجزئية لكونها في قوتها وتحتمل الكلية بناء على أن مهملات العلوم كليات .
[The Third Objective: Sound Inference According to the Majority] The one that includes its conditions according to its matter and form yields knowledge of the object of inference, according to the majority. As for its yielding opinion, it has been said that it is agreed upon by all. And it is necessary before beginning... His saying: 'that includes its conditions etc.' He explained the soundness of inference by what is its cause, so that the ruling that it yields knowledge is established without doubt; rather, it is self-evident according to what he transmitted from Nihāyat al-ʿUqūl. Not because there is no interpretation of it as an attribute; for there is no hiddenness in the correctness of our statement: 'inference that leads to obtaining the desired conclusion yields knowledge of it in general.' And because if it were otherwise, its division into the two divisions according to leading and not leading would be futile. His saying: 'agreed upon etc.' because if it were not also useful for opinion, it would not lead to obtaining the desired conclusion at all, neither knowledge nor opinion, so it would not be sound. His saying: 'and it is necessary etc.' for what was mentioned previously is a general statement that admits particularity because it is in its force, and admits universality based on the fact that the general statements of the sciences are universal.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines sound inference according to the majority (al-jumhūr). The definition is: 'the one that includes its conditions according to its matter and form yields knowledge of the object of inference.' The commentator explains that 'soundness' here is defined by its cause (the conditions of matter and form) to avoid circularity—if we said 'sound inference is that which leads to the desired conclusion' then saying that it yields knowledge would be tautological. Instead, the definition specifies the conditions that cause soundness, making the claim that it yields knowledge self-evident. The passage also notes that the majority agree that sound inference yields opinion as well; if it did not even yield opinion, it would not lead to any cognition. Finally, it remarks that the earlier statement 'it yields knowledge' is a general statement (muhmala) that can be interpreted as particular or universal, but in the sciences such statements are treated as universal.

Key Terms
نظر صحيح (sound inference)
جمهور (majority)
مادة (matter)
صورة (form)
علم (knowledge)
ظن (opinion)
شرائط (conditions)
بديهي (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of sound inference is circular: 'sound inference is that which yields knowledge.'
Correction: The author avoids circularity by defining sound inference by its conditions (matter and form) rather than by its effect. This makes the claim that it yields knowledge a self-evident consequence, not a tautology.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator explain that sound inference is defined by its conditions of matter and form rather than by 'leading to the desired conclusion'?
A) To avoid circularity and make the claim that it yields knowledge self-evident.
B) Because matter and form are easier to verify.
C) Because only the majority accept this definition.
D) Because the definition must include both matter and form.

Key Takeaway
Sound inference is defined by its conditions (matter and form) to avoid circularity, and it necessarily yields knowledge or at least opinion.

Definition and Conditions of Sound Reasoning
Sharh clarifies the definition of sound reasoning and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sound reasoning is defined by its cause and not merely as a description, and how its conditions ensure it yields knowledge or at least opinion.

Core Idea
Sound reasoning is defined by its cause so that its knowledge-yielding power is self-evident.

The Sharh clarifies the matn's definition of sound reasoning. It explains that the definition identifies the cause of soundness, not just a description, to make the judgment of yielding knowledge irresistible. This passage also addresses the role of opinion and stresses the need to specify the exact point of dispute before inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first a clarification of the definition, then a remark on opinion, then a note on methodology.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand why the definition is by cause
The Sharh states that soundness is interpreted 'by what is its cause' so that the outcome (knowledge) follows without doubt, even becoming self-evident. Ask yourself: why is this move necessary?
It avoids circularity: if sound reasoning were simply 'that which leads to the desired result', dividing reasoning into leading/not leading would be pointless.
3
Grasp the condition about opinion
The passage notes that sound reasoning must at least yield opinion. If it did not even give opinion, it would not lead to the desired result at all. This shows a lower threshold for soundness.
This is a point of agreement among scholars.
4
Appreciate the need to define the point of dispute
Before inquiry, one must define the exact issue. The earlier phrase about conditions is a general statement (muhmala) that can be taken as particular or universal; in sciences, such generalities are treated as universals.
This ensures clarity and avoids ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المشتمل على شرائطه إلخ) فسر صحة النظر بما هو سببها ليترتب الحكم عليه بإفادته للعلم من غير شبهة بل يكون بديهياً على ما نقله من نهاية العقول لا لأنه لا تفسيرها بما هو صفة؛ إذ لا خفاء في صحة قولنا النظر الذي يؤدي إلى حصول المطلوب يفيد العلم به في الجملة ولأنه لو كان كذلك لكان تقسيمه إلى القسمين باعتبار التأدية وعدمها عبثاً. قوله : (متفق عليها إلخ) لأنه لو لم يكن مفيداً للظن أيضاً لم يكن مؤدياً إلى حصول المطلوب أصلاً لا علماً ولا ظناً فلا يكون صحيحاً قوله : ( ولا بد إلخ) فإن المذكور سابقاً مهملة تحتمل الجزئية لكونها في قوتها وتحتمل الكلية بناء على أن مهملات العلوم كليات .
He said: '(Which includes its conditions according to its matter and form) he interpreted the soundness of reasoning by that which is its cause, so that the judgment that it yields knowledge follows without doubt, but rather it becomes self-evident as transmitted from Nihayat al-'Uqul — not because there is no interpretation of it as being a description; for there is no obscurity in the correctness of our statement: "Reasoning that leads to the attainment of the desired result yields knowledge of it in general." And because if it were otherwise, its division into two kinds with respect to leading and not leading would be futile. He said: '(And as for its leading to opinion, it has been said that it is agreed upon by all)' — because if it did not also lead to opinion, it would not lead to the attainment of the desired result at all, neither knowledge nor opinion, so it would not be sound. He said: '(And it is necessary) before beginning [inquiry]' — for what was mentioned earlier is a general (muhmala) that admits of particularity, because it is in its force, and admits of universality, based on the fact that the generalities of the sciences are universals.'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the definition of sound reasoning (al-nazar al-sahih) according to the majority. The Sharh clarifies that the base text's description of sound reasoning as that which 'includes its conditions according to its matter and form' is not a mere description but identifies the cause (sabab) of its soundness. The purpose is to ensure that the judgment that it yields knowledge follows without any doubt, to the point of being self-evident (badihi). The Sharh then addresses why this definition is not circular: if it were simply 'that which leads to the desired result,' then dividing reasoning into leading and not leading would be pointless. Next, the Sharh explains the statement that sound reasoning yields opinion by consensus: if it did not even yield opinion, it would not lead to the desired result at all, thus it would not be sound. Finally, the Sharh comments on the need to define the point of dispute before beginning inquiry, noting that the earlier phrase (the conditions) is a general statement that could be interpreted as either particular or universal, and the context of scientific discourse treats such generalities as universals.

Al-Amidi's Definition of Sound Nazar
Al-Amidi defines sound nazar with four conditions and responds to an objection about conceptual reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's definition of sound nazar and the conditions that restrict it, including why conceptual reasoning is not its primary subject.

Core Idea
Sound nazar is correct in matter and form, in certainties, and must not be followed by its opposite.

Al-Amidi begins by laying down the precise conditions for valid theological reasoning. His definition carefully excludes probable premises and ensures that sound reasoning yields certain knowledge. The passage also addresses an important objection about conceptual reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Notice the key phrases: 'بحسب مادته وصورته معاً', 'في القطعيات', 'لا يعقبه ضد للعلم', 'مفيد له'.
Each condition restricts the scope.
2
Understand why probable premises are excluded
The condition 'fi al-qat'iyyat' ensures only certain premises produce knowledge, not opinion.
3
Analyze the condition 'la ya'qibuhu didd li al-'ilm'
This means sound nazar must not be followed by anything that prevents knowledge, like death or heedlessness.
The commentator later explains this in more detail.
4
Examine the objection about conceptual reasoning
The objector says sound nazar in tasawwurat is not in certainties, so it is excluded. The response says the main focus is tasdiqiyyat.
This shows the definition is primarily for judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقيناً وقال الآمدي: كل نظر صحيح بحسب مادته وصورته معاً في القطعيات، احترز بهذا القيد عن النظر الصحيح الذي في المقدمات الظنية الصادقة، فإنه يفيد ظناً لا علماً، لا يعقبه ضد للعلم أي مناف له، كالموت والنوم والغفلة، وفائدة هذا التقييد ظاهرة، مفيد له أي للعلم، فقد جعل المدعى موجبة كلية موضوعها مقيد بقيود. فإن قلت: الأنظار الصحيحة في التصورات ليست واقعة في القطعيات فلا تندرج في هذه الموجبة الكلية، قلت: لا بأس بذلك فإن المقصد الأصلي هو الأنظار التصديقية لأن حالها في الإفادة مما علم يقيناً. وفي نهاية العقول: أن من عرف حقيقة النظر الذي يدعي أنه يفضي إلى العلم علم بالضرورة كونه كذلك، فإنا نعني بالنظر ما يتضمن مجموع علوم أربعة: الأول العلم بالمقدمات المرتبة، الثاني العلم بصحة ترتيبها، الثالث العلم بلزوم المطلوب عن تلك المقدمات المعلومة صحتها وصحة ترتيبها، الرابع العلم بأن ما علم لزومه عن تلك المقدمات كان صحيحاً، ولا شك أن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة العقل أن من حصلت له هذه العلوم الأربعة فلا بد من أن يحصل العلم بصحة المطلوب. هذا محصول كلامه. وحاصله أن من تصور النظر من حيث أنه مادة وصورة ولاحظ معه حال اللازم منه بالقياس إليه جزم بأن كل نظر صحيح يستلزم العلم جزماً بديهياً لا يحتاج فيه إلى تعقل الطرفين على الوجه الذي هو صحيح مناط. قوله: (لأن حالها في الإفادة إلخ) بخلاف الأنظار الواقعة في التصورات فإن في إفادتها شبهة، ولذا أنكرها الإمام. قوله: (وفي نهاية العقول إلخ) تأييد لقوله لا بأس بذلك بأن الإمام أيضاً خص بالأنظار التصديقية لكن يمكن أن يقال: إن تخصيصه بها لإنكاره الأنظار التصويرية. قوله: (علم بالضرورة) أي بالبديهة حيث رتبه على مجرد عرفان حقيقة النظر، وإنما لم يتعرض لتصور المحمول على ما هو مناط الحكم لعدم الخفاء فيه. قوله: (فإنا نعني بالنظر) أي بمعرفته كما يدل عليه السابق واللاحق. قوله: (ما يتضمن مجموع علوم أربعة) تضمن معرفة حقيقة النظر الصحيح للعلوم الثلاثة ظاهر، إذ لا معنى للصحيح إلا ذلك، وأما العلم الرابع فخارج عن حقيقة النظر مستفاد من مقدمة صادقة معلومة لنا حقيقة وهو لازم الحق حق، وإلا لبطل اللزوم فلعله أراد بالتضمن الاستتباع فإن هذا العلم تابع في الحصول لتلك العلوم الثلاثة. ليس ضداً للعلم لأن استحالة اجتماعهما ليست لذاتيهما بل لفوات شرط العلم، وهو الحياة بالموت، لأنا نقول: لو صح هذا لامتنع التضاد مطلقاً، إذ ما من شيء يقدر بينهما تضاد إلا ويمكن أن يقال: امتناع الجمع بينهما ليس لذاتيهما بل لفوات شرط أحدهما وهو خلاف الإجماع. قوله: (وفي نهاية العقول قيل: فائدة نقل هذا الكلام تقويمه الجواب المذكور ببيان أن الإمام أيضاً صرح بالأنظار التصديقية، والتنبيه على أنه كما بالجزئية صرح بالكلية أيضاً، ثم إن مراده بالضرورة في قوله علم بالضرورة كونه كذلك هو البداهة لا مجرد القطع واليقين بقرينة قولهم: ولا شك أن كل عاقل يعلم ببداهة العقل إلخ واشتراطه تلخيص تصور الموضوع، أعني النظر على ما هو مناط للحكم قرينة على ذلك أيضاً. قوله: (فإنا نعني بالنظر) أي بمعرفة معنى النظر.
(In certainty) and al-Amidi said: Every sound reasoning (nazar) according to its matter and form together (in certain matters) is guarded by this restriction from sound reasoning that occurs in true probable premises, for it yields opinion not knowledge, it is not followed by an opposite of knowledge i.e. a contradictory; like death, sleep, and heedlessness; and the benefit of this restriction is apparent, it is beneficial for knowledge; so he made the claim a universal affirmative whose subject is restricted by restrictions. Then if you say: Sound reasonings in conceptualizations (tasawwurat) are not located in certainties, so they are not included in this universal affirmative; I say: No problem with that, for the primary purpose is the tasdiqiyyat (judgments) because their state in producing knowledge is known with certainty. And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul: That whoever knows the reality of reasoning that claims to lead to knowledge knows necessarily that it is so, for we mean by reasoning what includes a total of four knowledges: first, knowledge of the ordered premises; second, knowledge of the correctness of their arrangement; third, knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion from those premises known to be correct in correctness and arrangement; fourth, knowledge that what is known to be necessary from those premises is correct. And there is no doubt that every rational person knows by the intuition of the intellect that whoever obtains these four knowledges must obtain knowledge of the correctness of the desired conclusion. This is the summary of his statement. And its gist is that whoever conceives reasoning as matter and form and observes with it the state of the necessary from it in relation to it, judges that every sound reasoning necessarily entails knowledge intuitively, not needing to contemplate the two extremes in the way that is the correct criterion. His statement: (Because their state in producing etc.) contrary to reasonings occurring in conceptualizations, for in their production there is doubt, and therefore the Imam rejected them. His statement: (And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul etc.) supports his statement 'no problem' in that the Imam also specified the tasdiqiyyat, but it can be said: His specification of them is due to his rejection of conceptual reasoning. His statement: (Knows necessarily) i.e. intuitively, since he based it on mere cognition of the reality of reasoning, and he did not address the conception of the predicate which is the criterion of judgment because of its lack of obscurity. His statement: (For we mean by reasoning) i.e. by its cognition, as indicated by what precedes and follows. His statement: (What includes a total of four knowledges) includes knowledge of the reality of sound reasoning for the three knowledges clearly, for the meaning of 'sound' is nothing but that; as for the fourth knowledge, it is outside the reality of reasoning, derived from a true premise known to us in reality, and it is 'the necessary of truth is true', otherwise necessity would be invalid; perhaps he meant by 'inclusion' entailment, for this knowledge is subsequent in attainment to those three knowledges. It is not an opposite of knowledge because the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to their essences but due to the loss of a condition of knowledge, which is life by death; for we say: If this were correct, opposition would be impossible absolutely, for there is no thing in which opposition is estimated between them except that it can be said: The impossibility of combining them is not due to their essences but due to the loss of a condition of one of them, which is contrary to consensus. His statement: (And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul it was said: The benefit of transmitting this statement is to strengthen the mentioned answer by showing that the Imam also explicitly stated the tasdiqiyyat, and a reminder that just as he explicitly stated the particularity, he also explicitly stated the universality; then his intention by 'necessary' in his statement 'knows necessarily' is intuition, not mere certainty and certainty, by the evidence of their statement: 'And no doubt that every rational person knows by the intuition of the intellect etc.' and his condition of summarizing the conception of the subject, i.e. reasoning, as the criterion for judgment is also evidence of that. His statement: (For we mean by reasoning) i.e. by knowing the meaning of reasoning.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a complex definition, explanation, and defense of sound reasoning (nazar) as understood by the majority of theologians. It begins with al-Amidi's definition: sound nazar is correct in both its matter (premises) and form (logical structure), applies only to certainties (qat'iyyat), is not followed by an opposite of knowledge (like death, sleep, or heedlessness), and produces knowledge. The commentator then explains each qualifying condition: the restriction 'fi al-qat'iyyat' excludes probable premises, which yield only opinion. The condition 'la ya'qibuhu didd li al-'ilm' is interpreted as meaning no contradictory arises that prevents knowledge; death is given as an example, but a later note clarifies that death is not a true opposite of knowledge because its incompatibility is due to a missing condition (life), not essence. The phrase 'mufid lahu' means the reasoning must actually yield knowledge. The definition is formulated as a universal proposition with a restricted subject. An objection is then raised: sound reasoning in conceptual matters (tasawwurat) does not fall under certainties, so the definition seems to exclude them. The response is that the primary concern is judgments (tasdiqiyyat), not concepts; conceptual reasoning is not the main goal because it does not produce certainty in the same way. This is supported by a quotation from 'Nihayat al-‘Uqul' (attributed to Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni), where nazar is analyzed as comprising four knowledges: knowledge of the ordered premises, knowledge of the correctness of their arrangement, knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion from those premises, and knowledge that what is thus necessitated is correct. The combination of these four is said to lead to certain knowledge intuitively. The commentator then explains that this fourfold analysis presupposes that the matter and form of nazar are sound, and that the fourth knowledge (the truth of the necessary conclusion) is derived from an external principle that the necessary consequence of truth is true. A further note addresses the condition regarding opposites: death is not a true opposite of knowledge because the impossibility of coexistence is due to a missing condition (life), not essential opposition. This is supported by an appeal to consensus that if that were allowed, all opposition could be explained away. Finally, a comment on the Nihayah quotation emphasizes that the Imam's point is to strengthen the response and clarify that the term 'necessity' in 'knows necessarily' refers to intuition (badiha), not mere conviction. Throughout, the passage shows the careful nuanced reasoning typical of kalam methodology.

Al-Amidi defines sound nazar as reasoning that is correct in matter and form, confined to certainties, not followed by an opposite, and productive of knowledge, with conceptual reasoning being secondary.

The Four Knowledges of Nazar and the Nature of Opposition
Al-Amidi analyzes nazar into four knowledges and clarifies the condition about the opposite of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analysis of nazar into four knowledges from Nihayat al-‘Uqul and the clarification of the condition regarding the opposite of knowledge.

Core Idea
Sound nazar entails four knowledges that together yield certain knowledge intuitively.

The response to the objection is further supported by a passage from Nihayat al-‘Uqul, which analyzes sound nazar into four essential knowledges. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'opposite of knowledge' and defends it against a potential misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Nihayah quotation
Understand the four knowledges listed as necessary for nazar to produce knowledge.
These four cover premises, arrangement, necessity, and correctness.
2
See how these four guarantee certainty
The author argues that anyone who possesses these four knowledges will intuitively know the correctness of the conclusion.
3
Examine the discussion on opposites
The condition 'not followed by an opposite' is clarified: death is not a true opposite because its incompatibility is due to a missing condition (life).
This nuance prevents universal denial of opposition.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
This passage reinforces that sound nazar is a rigorous process that leads to irrefutable certainty.

حال اللازم منه بالقياس إليه جزم بأن كل نظر صحيح يستلزم العلم جزماً بديهياً لا يحتاج فيه إلى تعقل الطرفين على الوجه الذي هو صحيح مناط. قوله: (لأن حالها في الإفادة إلخ) بخلاف الأنظار الواقعة في التصورات فإن في إفادتها شبهة، ولذا أنكرها الإمام. قوله: (وفي نهاية العقول إلخ) تأييد لقوله لا بأس بذلك بأن الإمام أيضاً خص بالأنظار التصديقية لكن يمكن أن يقال: إن تخصيصه بها لإنكاره الأنظار التصويرية. قوله: (علم بالضرورة) أي بالبديهة حيث رتبه على مجرد عرفان حقيقة النظر، وإنما لم يتعرض لتصور المحمول على ما هو مناط الحكم لعدم الخفاء فيه. قوله: (فإنا نعني بالنظر) أي بمعرفته كما يدل عليه السابق واللاحق. قوله: (ما يتضمن مجموع علوم أربعة) تضمن معرفة حقيقة النظر الصحيح للعلوم الثلاثة ظاهر، إذ لا معنى للصحيح إلا ذلك، وأما العلم الرابع فخارج عن حقيقة النظر مستفاد من مقدمة صادقة معلومة لنا حقيقة وهو لازم الحق حق، وإلا لبطل اللزوم فلعله أراد بالتضمن الاستتباع فإن هذا العلم تابع في الحصول لتلك العلوم الثلاثة. ليس ضداً للعلم لأن استحالة اجتماعهما ليست لذاتيهما بل لفوات شرط العلم، وهو الحياة بالموت، لأنا نقول: لو صح هذا لامتنع التضاد مطلقاً، إذ ما من شيء يقدر بينهما تضاد إلا ويمكن أن يقال: امتناع الجمع بينهما ليس لذاتيهما بل لفوات شرط أحدهما وهو خلاف الإجماع. قوله: (وفي نهاية العقول قيل: فائدة نقل هذا الكلام تقويمه الجواب المذكور ببيان أن الإمام أيضاً صرح بالأنظار التصديقية، والتنبيه على أنه كما بالجزئية صرح بالكلية أيضاً، ثم إن مراده بالضرورة في قوله علم بالضرورة كونه كذلك هو البداهة لا مجرد القطع واليقين بقرينة قولهم: ولا شك أن كل عاقل يعلم ببداهة العقل إلخ واشتراطه تلخيص تصور الموضوع، أعني النظر على ما هو مناط للحكم قرينة على ذلك أيضاً. قوله: (فإنا نعني بالنظر) أي بمعرفة معنى النظر.
(In certainty) and al-Amidi said: Every sound reasoning (nazar) according to its matter and form together (in certain matters) is guarded by this restriction from sound reasoning that occurs in true probable premises, for it yields opinion not knowledge, it is not followed by an opposite of knowledge i.e. a contradictory; like death, sleep, and heedlessness; and the benefit of this restriction is apparent, it is beneficial for knowledge; so he made the claim a universal affirmative whose subject is restricted by restrictions. Then if you say: Sound reasonings in conceptualizations (tasawwurat) are not located in certainties, so they are not included in this universal affirmative; I say: No problem with that, for the primary purpose is the tasdiqiyyat (judgments) because their state in producing knowledge is known with certainty. And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul: That whoever knows the reality of reasoning that claims to lead to knowledge knows necessarily that it is so, for we mean by reasoning what includes a total of four knowledges: first, knowledge of the ordered premises; second, knowledge of the correctness of their arrangement; third, knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion from those premises known to be correct in correctness and arrangement; fourth, knowledge that what is known to be necessary from those premises is correct. And there is no doubt that every rational person knows by the intuition of the intellect that whoever obtains these four knowledges must obtain knowledge of the correctness of the desired conclusion. This is the summary of his statement. And its gist is that whoever conceives reasoning as matter and form and observes with it the state of the necessary from it in relation to it, judges that every sound reasoning necessarily entails knowledge intuitively, not needing to contemplate the two extremes in the way that is the correct criterion. His statement: (Because their state in producing etc.) contrary to reasonings occurring in conceptualizations, for in their production there is doubt, and therefore the Imam rejected them. His statement: (And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul etc.) supports his statement 'no problem' in that the Imam also specified the tasdiqiyyat, but it can be said: His specification of them is due to his rejection of conceptual reasoning. His statement: (Knows necessarily) i.e. intuitively, since he based it on mere cognition of the reality of reasoning, and he did not address the conception of the predicate which is the criterion of judgment because of its lack of obscurity. His statement: (For we mean by reasoning) i.e. by its cognition, as indicated by what precedes and follows. His statement: (What includes a total of four knowledges) includes knowledge of the reality of sound reasoning for the three knowledges clearly, for the meaning of 'sound' is nothing but that; as for the fourth knowledge, it is outside the reality of reasoning, derived from a true premise known to us in reality, and it is 'the necessary of truth is true', otherwise necessity would be invalid; perhaps he meant by 'inclusion' entailment, for this knowledge is subsequent in attainment to those three knowledges. It is not an opposite of knowledge because the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to their essences but due to the loss of a condition of knowledge, which is life by death; for we say: If this were correct, opposition would be impossible absolutely, for there is no thing in which opposition is estimated between them except that it can be said: The impossibility of combining them is not due to their essences but due to the loss of a condition of one of them, which is contrary to consensus. His statement: (And in Nihayat al-‘Uqul it was said: The benefit of transmitting this statement is to strengthen the mentioned answer by showing that the Imam also explicitly stated the tasdiqiyyat, and a reminder that just as he explicitly stated the particularity, he also explicitly stated the universality; then his intention by 'necessary' in his statement 'knows necessarily' is intuition, not mere certainty and certainty, by the evidence of their statement: 'And no doubt that every rational person knows by the intuition of the intellect etc.' and his condition of summarizing the conception of the subject, i.e. reasoning, as the criterion for judgment is also evidence of that. His statement: (For we mean by reasoning) i.e. by knowing the meaning of reasoning.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a complex definition, explanation, and defense of sound reasoning (nazar) as understood by the majority of theologians. It begins with al-Amidi's definition: sound nazar is correct in both its matter (premises) and form (logical structure), applies only to certainties (qat'iyyat), is not followed by an opposite of knowledge (like death, sleep, or heedlessness), and produces knowledge. The commentator then explains each qualifying condition: the restriction 'fi al-qat'iyyat' excludes probable premises, which yield only opinion. The condition 'la ya'qibuhu didd li al-'ilm' is interpreted as meaning no contradictory arises that prevents knowledge; death is given as an example, but a later note clarifies that death is not a true opposite of knowledge because its incompatibility is due to a missing condition (life), not essence. The phrase 'mufid lahu' means the reasoning must actually yield knowledge. The definition is formulated as a universal proposition with a restricted subject. An objection is then raised: sound reasoning in conceptual matters (tasawwurat) does not fall under certainties, so the definition seems to exclude them. The response is that the primary concern is judgments (tasdiqiyyat), not concepts; conceptual reasoning is not the main goal because it does not produce certainty in the same way. This is supported by a quotation from 'Nihayat al-‘Uqul' (attributed to Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni), where nazar is analyzed as comprising four knowledges: knowledge of the ordered premises, knowledge of the correctness of their arrangement, knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion from those premises, and knowledge that what is thus necessitated is correct. The combination of these four is said to lead to certain knowledge intuitively. The commentator then explains that this fourfold analysis presupposes that the matter and form of nazar are sound, and that the fourth knowledge (the truth of the necessary conclusion) is derived from an external principle that the necessary consequence of truth is true. A further note addresses the condition regarding opposites: death is not a true opposite of knowledge because the impossibility of coexistence is due to a missing condition (life), not essential opposition. This is supported by an appeal to consensus that if that were allowed, all opposition could be explained away. Finally, a comment on the Nihayah quotation emphasizes that the Imam's point is to strengthen the response and clarify that the term 'necessity' in 'knows necessarily' refers to intuition (badiha), not mere conviction. Throughout, the passage shows the careful nuanced reasoning typical of kalam methodology.

Correction: Death is not an opposite of knowledge because the impossibility of their coexistence is due to a missing condition (life), not essential opposition.

Key Takeaway

?

The Four Sciences of Correct Speculative Reasoning
al-Jurjani defines nazar as four sciences and argues it yields necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that correct speculative reasoning (nazar) is defined as a composite of four specific sciences, and that it necessarily yields knowledge by intellectual intuition.

Core Idea
Correct speculation is not just thinking—it is a structured set of four knowledges that guarantee knowledge.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, al-Jurjani presents a precise definition of correct speculative reasoning (nazar). He argues that anyone who understands what nazar truly is—a combination of four distinct sciences—will inevitably recognize that it produces certain knowledge. Let's explore his definition and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines nazar in terms of four sciences.
The passage quotes from Nihayat al-'Uqul.
2
Identify the four sciences
List the four sciences that together constitute nazar. Understand what each one adds to the reasoning process.
3
Understand the argument for necessary knowledge
See why the author claims that possessing these four sciences inevitably leads to knowledge of the conclusion. How does he justify this?
The argument appeals to intellectual intuition (badihah).
4
Reflect on the definition
Consider why nazar must include all four. What would happen if any were missing?

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : النظر الصحيح عند الجمهور

تندرج في هذه الموجبة الكلية، قلت: لا بأس بذلك فإن المقصد الأصلي هو الأنظار التصديقية لأن حالها في الإفادة مما علم يقيناً، وفي نهاية العقول : أن من عرف حقيقة النظر الذي يدعي أنه يفضي إلى العلم علم بالضرورة كونه كذلك، فإنا نعني بالنظر ما يتضمن مجموع علوم أربعة الأول العلم بالمقدمات المرتبة الثاني العلم بصحة ترتيبها، الثالث العلم بلزوم المطلوب عن تلك المقدمات المعلومة صحتها وصحة ترتيبها، الرابع العلم بأن ما علم لزومه عن تلك المقدمات كان صحيحاً، ولا شك أن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة العقل أن من حصلت له هذه العلوم الأربعة فلا بد من أن يحصل العلم بصحة المطلوب هذا محصول كلامه وحاصله أن من تصور النظر من حيث أنه مادة وصورة ولاحظ معه حال اللازم منه بالقياس إليه جزم بأن كل نظر صحيح يستلزم العلم جزماً بديهياً لا يحتاج فيه إلى تعقل الطرفين على الوجه الذي هو صحيح مناط
The Fifth Observatory - The Third Objective: Correct Speculative Reasoning According to the Majority

[This] is included in this universal affirmative. I say: There is no harm in that, for the original purpose is the assertoric speculations (al-andhar al-tasdiqiyya) because their case in providing benefit is from what is known with certainty. And in Nihayat al-'Uqul: Whoever knows the reality of speculation (nazar) which is claimed to lead to knowledge knows necessarily that it is so. For we mean by speculation that which comprises a totality of four sciences: first, knowledge of the ordered premises; second, knowledge of their correct ordering; third, knowledge of the necessary implication of the desired conclusion from those premises whose correctness and correct ordering are known; fourth, knowledge that what is known to be implied by those premises is correct. And there is no doubt that every rational person knows by the intuition of the intellect (badihah al-'aql) that whoever obtains these four sciences must obtain knowledge of the correctness of the desired conclusion. This is the gist of his words. And its summary is that whoever conceptualizes speculation (nazar) in terms of its matter and form, and observes with it the state of the necessary [conclusion] relative to it, judges with certainty that every correct speculation necessarily yields knowledge intuitively (badihan), without needing to conceptualize the two extremes in the manner that is the correct basis.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines correct speculative reasoning (nazar) and proves that it necessarily yields knowledge. The author begins by linking this discussion to a universal affirmative statement, then quotes from Nihayat al-'Uqul: knowledge of the true nature of nazar leads to the necessary conclusion that it produces knowledge. He defines nazar as comprising four types of knowledge: (1) knowledge of the premises arranged, (2) knowledge of their correct arrangement, (3) knowledge that the desired conclusion necessarily follows from those premises given their correctness and correct arrangement, and (4) knowledge that what is known to follow is correct. Everyone with intellect knows intuitively that one who possesses these four must obtain knowledge of the conclusion's truth. Thus, any correct nazar inevitably yields intuitive knowledge without needing to consider the two extremes separately.

Refuting the Charge of Circularity in Proving Universals through Particulars
The commentator refutes the charge of circularity in proving universals through particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving a universal judgment through a particular judgment does not lead to the fallacy of proving a thing by itself, due to the difference of aspect (ikhtilaf al-jiha).

Core Idea
Particulars prove universals without circularity because each has two aspects: intuitive and theoretical.

The commentator takes up an objection raised by Imam al-Razi: that any attempt to prove a universal rule through a specific instance would be circular, since the instance's own judgment is what we are trying to prove. The answer lies in carefully distinguishing two aspects of the particular judgment—its own specific essence and its role as an instance of the universal. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the charge of self-proof fails.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
The charge is that proving a universal via a particular implies self-proof (ithbat al-shay' bi nafsihi). Understand the problem before reading the answer.
This sets up the need for a careful distinction.
2
Grasp the key distinction
The particular judgment has two aspects: its own specific essence (individuating features) and its being a member of the universal. The first is known intuitively, the second theoretically.
This distinction is the core of the refutation.
3
Apply the difference of aspect
Because the particular is prover in one respect (as individual) and proved in another (as itself), there is no circularity. The intuitive aspect grounds the theoretical.
The concept of ikhtilaf al-jiha (difference of aspect) is crucial.
4
Examine the indefinite case
The commentator explains why the charge of self-proof only applies to universal judgments, not indefinite ones. For indefinite propositions, the danger is regress (tasalsul) rather than self-proof.
This clarifies the scope of the objection.
